A collaboration between the Vallejo, California, rapper and his family group the Click \u2014 brother B-Legit, sister Suga T, and cousin D-Shot \u2014 \u201cCaptain Save a Hoe\u201d is only one example of E-40\u2019s talent for inventing and\/or popularizing local slang (also see the deathless word \u201cd-boy\u201d) as well as his ability to balance P-funk-like satire and pointed reality rap. Even Suga T mocks the titular cornball: \u201cIf ya breath stank, ya gets ganked, mang.\u201d Meanwhile, producer Studio Ton arranges a slumping, bluesy beat full of funky worm melody and mobbed-out Though there were plenty of similar rap protests in the late Eighties \u2014 see Public Enemy\u2019s \u201cBlack Steel in the Hour of Chaos\u201d \u2014 none of them sounded as visceral as \u201cFuck tha Police.\u201d It led to years of harassment by law enforcement, and N.W.A was banned from performing it during their 1989 national tour, even as it grew into a cultural milestone and turned N.W.A into the most important musical group in the country. So much of Bay Area hip-hop culture has been built on what he made that summoning his unique style is as innate as the San Francisco Bay salt water that lingers in the breeze.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tLil Jon, whom Too Short connected with while living in Atlanta, produced \u201cBlow the Whistle.\u201d (He also produced E-40\u2019s hyphy classic \u201cTell Me When to Go,\u201d just one of many links between <a  href=\"https:\/\/www.kqed.org\/arts\/13925958\/bay-area-west-coast-south-rap-hip-hop-master-p-no-limit\"  rel=\"nofollow\"  target=\"_blank\"  >Bay Area and Southern rap<\/a>.) The track is vintage Short, all rubbery synth bass so thick it could break car speakers. He opens the track with the line, \u201cI go on and on,\u201d as if this is just the latest verse in a decades-long song. Of course, he also asks, \u201cWhat\u2019s my favorite word?\u201d But \u201cBlow the Whistle\u201d holds universal appeal and whether you\u2019re a hip-hop OG, a big stepper, or <a  href=\"https:\/\/www.theguardian.com\/world\/2014\/aug\/02\/bad-feminist-roxane-gay-extract\"  rel=\"nofollow\"  target=\"_blank\"  >a bad feminist<\/a>, you\u2019ll respond by answering Their debut album, <em>93 \u2018Til Infinity<\/em>, proved popular among hip-hop heads \u2013 A Tribe Called Quest included them them in the elite group of artists pictured on the cover of <em>Midnight Marauders <\/em>\u2014 but made little mainstream impact. The public was only becoming aware of his hundreds of unreleased songs; how did he complete this new album if he was dead? Had he prophesied his own death and rebirth by assuming the name Makaveli, a play on the Italian philosopher Niccol\u00f2 Machiavelli? class=\"lrv-u-text-transform-uppercase u-letter-spacing-012\">\u201cYou are now<\/span>  about to witness the strength of street knowledge,\u201d begins Eazy-E on \u201cStraight Outta Compton,\u201d the title track that kicks off N.W.A.\u2019s 1989 album. It\u2019s difficult to convey how electrifying and dangerous the song sounded upon its release, even to an audience familiar with the group\u2019s prior hits, \u201cDopeman\u201d and \u201c8 Ball,\u201d as well as the advance single from the album, \u201cGangsta Gangsta.\u201d It was one of two tracks the group was forbidden to play during their mythic 1989 national tour, the other being \u201cFuck tha Police,\u201d and MTV banned its video. It felt like a lightning strike, completely shorn of the thuggish whimsy that animated Eazy-E\u2019s solo work or the sinuously funky groove that made \u201cGangsta Gangsta\u201d a Black radio favorite. This was the one that made the white rock establishment, and not a few in the Black music community, tremble with fear and call the group glorified gangbangers.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tCo-produced by Dr. Dre and DJ Yella and inspired by the Bomb Squad\u2019s sample-heavy quilt <em>It Takes a Nation of Millions to Hold Us Back<\/em>, \u201cStraight Outta Compton\u201d is a gumbo of drum breaks, vocal snippets, and Yella\u2019s turntable scratches. Ice Cube comes out the gate with pure aggression and a \u201cmurder rap\u201d verse. He\u2019s followed by the self-described villain MC Ren, and Eazy-E, \u201cthe motherfucker raising hell.\u201d For all its velocity and lack of a clear chorus, the production is sophisticated, with a short bridge between the first two raps and Eazy\u2019s closing verse. Still, it\u2019s a cyclone that doesn\u2019t let up until the very end, when Dre calls out, \u201cDamn, that shit was dope!\u201d<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tIn 2015, the N.W.A. legend was mythologized in an Oscar-nominated movie, <em>Straight Outta Compton<\/em>. The group that once shook the foundation of the music industry was now a Rock and Roll Hall of Fame institution, their angst safely absorbed into the pop firmament. But the old heads who remember when \u201cStraight Outta Compton\u201d dropped won\u2019t forget that first impression.<em>&#8211;M.R.<\/em><\/p>\n<\/div>","alt":"N.W.A.","image_credit":"Al Snoop Doggy Dogg","subtitle":"1992","slug":"nuthin-but-a-g-thang-dr-dre-1234713597","caption":"","description":"<!-- do not apply CSS styles to this element! -->\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"pmc-not-a-paywall\">\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\t<span class=\"lrv-u-text-transform-uppercase u-letter-spacing-012\">\u201cNuthin\u2019 but a<\/span>  G Thang\u201d changed <em>everything<\/em>. It codified a sound that percolated on records such as N.W.A.\u2019s <em>efil4zaggaN <\/em>and DJ Quik\u2019s <em>Quik Is the Name<\/em> and made it the default style for Los Angeles past and present. It brought street culture to the forefront permanently, and forced every hip-hop artist to decide whether they were \u201cgangstas\u201d or not, with their careers hanging in the balance. And it made Dr. Dre the undisputed king of West Coast hip-hop for generations to come, casting a shadow that lingers to this day.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tDr. Dre had already introduced Long Beach rapper Snoop Doggy Dogg to the public earlier in 1992 on \u201cDeep Cover,\u201d a single from the movie soundtrack of the same name that caused a sensation in rap and angered law enforcement officials with Snoop\u2019s chorus, \u201cCause it\u2019s 1-8-7 on an undercover cop!\u201d Now, with the \u201cG Thang\u201d video blowing up on The Box during the holiday season, the duo delighted with their old-school tag-team flow and \u201crip shit up.\u201d Dre offered a sample of Leon Haywood\u2019s \u201cI Want\u2019 a Do Something Freaky to You,\u201d and created a vibe that felt familiar, as if the song had been out for years. The clip, directed by Dr. Dre, looks raw and unpolished as it depicts Dre picking up Snoop at his house, a barbecue in the park, and a house party.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tYet viewed today, \u201cNuthin\u2019 but a G Thang\u201d and its video also evokes troubling reactions. During a volleyball game at the barbecue, a man pulls down a woman\u2019s bikini top. More alarmingly, is the scene where a model makes her way into the house party. She declines the advances of numerous guys who try to approach her. Finally, annoyed by her apparent snobbishness, several dudes stage an assault by spraying malt liquor all over her.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tIt\u2019s important to point out that violence against women was a persistent theme during the hardcore early Nineties. These were years when MCs pushed lyricism into strange directions, exploring their inner mind\u2019s eye with little care for whether outsiders took offense. Empathizing with this approach was part of being a hip-hop consumer, even as artists pushed the boundaries of taste with fantasies of rape and murder.&nbsp;<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\tHowever, when Dre rapped, \u201cAnd if your bitches talk shit, I\u2019ll have to put the smack down,\u201d his words had a real-life parallel. His assault of rapper and television personality Dee Barnes in 1991 made national headlines; everyone knew who he wanted to \u201csmack down.\u201d To his credit, Dre has since taken steps to account for past behavior. \u201cI made some fucking horrible mistakes in my life,\u201d <a  href=\"https:\/\/www.rollingstone.com\/tv-movies\/tv-movie-news\/n-w-a-tell-all-inside-the-original-gangstas-rolling-stone-cover-story-67660\/\"  >he told Rolling Stone in 2015<\/a>. This year, Barnes <a  href=\"https:\/\/www.rollingstone.com\/music\/music-news\/dr-dre-grammy-award-dee-barnes-reacts-abuse-1234675681\/\"  >told <em>RS<\/em>\u2019s Mankaprr Contekh<\/a> that, in effect, she still hasn\u2019t forgiven him. And that\u2019s okay. It\u2019s her life that was irrevocably changed, and only she gets to decide how to deal with that.<\/p>\n\n\n\n<p\n\t\tclass=\"paragraph larva \/\/ lrv-u-line-height-copy  lrv-a-font-body-l   \"\n>\n\t\u201cNuthin\u2019 but a G Thang\u201d not only popularized what soon became known as \u201cG-funk,\u201d it also helped cement deep-rooted misogyny as an article of faith within rap music. Pointing out the latter doesn\u2019t minimize Dre and Snoop\u2019s historic achievement or that, simply put, they made an incredible, timeless piece of music. There\u2019s much to learn from this all-time classic as fans, the music industry, and the artists themselves reckon with the complications of the \u201cG Thang\u201d legacy.<em>&#8211;M.R.<\/em><\/p>\n<\/div>","alt":"Dr Dre","image_credit":"Ken Weingart\/Michael Ochs Archives\/Getty
